B00005Q4CS|5.0|Viewed: 7/07, 6/11<br />Rate: 10<br /><br />7/07: The movie starts off slowly in a dumb way that would have resembled an ordinary picture, but its freefall into despair is extraordinary. The infusion of style through Requiem for a Dream makes it an unforgettable picture. Quite possibly Requiem for a Dream may be the most haunting picture I've seen. The performances, save for Marlon Wayans, are thoroughly outstanding. There have been some pictures that deal with drug use and chemical dependency, and quite many of them glorify the shattering experience. But Requiem for a Dream is the exception. It elevates the elements of Sid and Nancy while incorporating the motivation with regard for neither nihilism nor gratification. No question, the most outstanding performance of the picture is Ellen Burstyn as she combines the delicacy of Vivien Leigh from A Streetcar Named Desire with the madness of Bette Davis from What Ever Happened to Baby Jane? Jared Leto is an actor of limited abilities, but in this one, he digs deep and pushes himself farther than he has never been. For Jennifer Connelly, she is usually a blank person with a lot of faÃ§ade that turned in many mediocre performances, but in this one, she fits in flawlessly of her role and transforms herself into the character that blended in what the content demands. A veteran of many bad, bad movies, Marlon Wayans makes an appearance in a film that's quite unlike him, and he does well staying within the level of others. Is it the performances that make Requiem for a Dream the picture it is, or is it the work of a director? Hmm, I think it's more of the latter. Darren Aronofsky shows that he had learned a great deal from his first picture Pi and transfers its style into Requiem for a Dream plus adding more inventive ways to take his vision to the next level. The result is a smashing one. It starts off, as I said, leisurely, begins its ascent with endearing performances, and lights up a fuse that promises to explode with lots of fireworks. When all are said and done, the effect of the picture has stayed with me mentally because it becomes, to a certain extent, impacting. Requiem for a Dream is a film that is waiting to be recognized as a venerable classic in decades to come, and it deserves to be held in high esteem. It's so unlike anything I've seen. I think that the movie had enough to make a compelling in-look of what would happen to a person if s/he started to get into hardcore drugs. Yeah, Traffic fails to portray it because Steven Soderbergh spent his time excessively juggling with so many characters in an overly ambitious picture. Darren Aronofsky avoids that mistake by keeping the characters small and simple. It is also at the point that their past backgrounds ceased to matter, so I am instructed to watch the decaying effects of the present characters to the point of finality. I must say, the title Requiem for a Dream is very intriguing. To dream of something big is to swap for something else just like how Dr. Faustus did? Seems to be like it. Some of the scenes in Requiem for a Dream are painful for me to watch, but I saw every second of it. When I could think of the most hollowing part, it has to be either the black circular bruise on Jared Leto's arm or the double sodomy sex scene in a machine gun rapidity. Giving the film a `10', it is easy for me to do so because when a film fails to invoke feelings, then it is not considered a good picture. But Requiem for a Dream pretty much takes away all of that from me, and I may have been speechless already afterwards. Surely, to show evidence, the fact is I usually write a review the day after I finish the movie, but for Requiem for a Dream, it is three days after. I may have to wait years before I will take interest in watching it again. To repeat, Ellen Burstyn's performance may be just the best of her career.<br /><br />6/11: Requiem for a Dream is the ultimate anti-drug picture. Show this to kids, and you'll get the desired effect because I don't know, except for junkies, who can not be repelled by Jared Leto's black circled arm. Also, it is the ultimate ADD picture because of the extensive editing where an average film would have 600 to 700 cuts, but Requiem for a Dream had 2,000 of it. I just love the camera style throughout; it's bold and innovative. The pacing is extremely fast and to the point, magnifying the extremeness per scene by 10 fold. As a result, the ending is one depressing maniac finish. Although the cast does very well collectively, it's Ellen Burstyn's show. She steals every scene and makes for one sad, pathetic character. If there is a reason not to do drugs, legal or illegal, and then abuse them, see Requiem for a Dream. It's worth every second. Forget the idea that there are extreme scenes; the film is meant to tackle an extreme subject in an extreme way. All in all, Requiem for a Dream is forever a classic.know, except for junkies, who can not be repelled by Jared Leto's black circled arm. Many deals are made.  There is a new political enemy for every friend a character makes. There are some differences between the two, but in my opinion, the differences are minor and do nothing to change the story.  As an example, there are several characters who look different in the show than they are described in the book.  It really didn't bother me at all.  I was really more concerned with the actors being able to portray the characters the way they were described, and I felt they were mostly on the money in that regard.  There were also a few scenes (Daenerys's wedding night for example) where the show exaggerated the circumstances a little bit, likely for visual impact, but that didn't bother me either.  In the grand scheme of things, I felt that the show was truly the living version of the book.  And lastly, the tv series has all of the kids being a couple years older in the beginning of the series than they are in the book.  This part was necessary because the actors can't age on demand and need to be able to keep up with their characters over time.  Honestly, I didn't even notice the difference.<br /><br />If you are a fan of fantasy books like The Lord of the Rings Trilogy, this series will be right up your alley. B000OIBY4Y|5.0|When I first started reading `The Stranger' by Albert Camus it seemed rather dull. It's a first person account from a somewhat bland character named Meursault, the titular `Stranger'. While working my way through the book I had to wonder if an alternate translation, `The Outsider', would be more appropriate for `L'&Eacute;tranger'. Meursault is a Frenchmen living on Algeria but in no way is he a stranger. He has a circle of friends, a job and even a girlfriend. What sets him apart from humanity is his possibly pathological indifference to just about anything whether it be abuse of a dog, abuse of a woman or even the death of his own mother. Not that he engages in abuse it's just that he seems unaffected by the suffering of others. Other descriptions I've read on this book have described Meursault as honest to a fault with this being his downfall. I'm not sure that gives people the correct impression. Meursault's honesty is not the kind where you tell a fat woman she's fat. His downfall is more his inability to feign sorrow, regret or empathy. When his girlfriend asks if he loves her he considers it and answers \\"no\\" without any thought that the answer might be painful to hear. About half way through the book, in a bizarre set of circumstances, Meursault ends up killing a man and when asked by the police if he feels regret he says he never looks on the past with regret and in this case feels only vexation. There is no evident malice only utter insensitivity.<br /><br />Philosophically The Stranger is one of the most intriguing and moving books I have ever read particularly the final act where Meursault confronts the priest who attempts to lead him to the Christian God in the last days before his execution. Despite the perceived indifference he exhibits throughout the book Meursault has a consistent and well defined philosophy of existence. In this moment Meursault disgorges everything he has on the hapless priest and lays bare his soul (so to speak). Knowing that his death is but weeks, days or perhaps hours away, he achieves a moment of clarity seeing his place in the universe, a universe even more indifferent than himself. Camus never absolves him of his crime but in a sense Meursault rises above the simple act of killing a man, above his imprisonment and above life itself. He achieves full acceptance of his existence and place in the universe and in that moment transcends life and God. I`m genuinely saddened that I'm not able to read the final chapter in its original French. If the translation is this good I can hardly imagine how amazing the original must be.<br /><br />This is the kind of book that one could read and ponder over and over again and I have a feeling I will. There is a considerable amount of symbolism throughout particularly the scorching sun which seems to continually oppress Meursault until he can take it no more. It starts off very slowly and builds throughout. I've never been on trial and certainly never been on death row but Camus gave Meursault an inner dialogue that rang so true it felt more real than any other portrayal I've seen or read. Despite his crime and often callous view of the suffering of others Camus created a character so real and open to the reader that I couldn't help but pity him terribly for his situation but in the end Meursault found peace regardless of the outcome. If you haven't read this book you really should and it's a short read so if you don't find it as profound as I did at least you wont have to endure it for long.death is but weeks, days or perhaps hours away, he achieves a moment of clarity seeing his place in the universe, a universe even more indifferent than himself. He achieves full acceptance of his existence and place in the universe and in that moment transcends life and God. If the translation is this good I can hardly imagine how amazing the original must be.<br /><br />This is the kind of book that one could read and ponder over and over again and I have a feeling I will. There is a considerable amount of symbolism throughout particularly the scorching sun which seems to continually oppress Meursault until he can take it no more. B004WPYNY8|5.0|Though I came into the Symphony X fold rather late in the game, they have quickly become one of my absolute favorites in the ever diversifying metal genre.  Because Paradise Lost was my introductory album (and still my favorite), I do have a slightly greater affinity for the less symphonic Symphony X of recent years.  But it must be said that I do count The Divine Wings of Tragedy and The Odyssey as my other top X picks.  But regardless of the direction that the band is taking, the juggernaut that is Allen/Romeo is as unstoppable as ever.  Not that the rest of the band is relegated to obscurity, as they are all at the top of their game for this album.  Symphony X has always been about the group as a whole, and the musicianship these guys portray is nothing less than stellar.<br /><br />As for the LP itself, never let it be said that Symphony X can't think big.  This Deluxe Edition album is simply SPRAWLING...12 tracks spread across 2 discs (nearly an hour and a half of music!), with precise production values and a man vs. machine concept that connects the narrative.  Not surprisingly, one of the greatest strengths of Iconoclast is the mixing/production.  Everything is crystal clear; from the toms & cymbals to the solos and even the bass guitar, which is frequently subdued or lost entirely on other band's records.  Nothing is \\"lost in the mix\\" here, and the album is that much stronger for it.  Also, the progressive elements, somewhat underplayed on the last album, have made a grand return here, with many of the songs having significant shifts in time and tone.  As for the lyrics, where Paradise Lost focused loosely on biblical themes, Iconoclast rarely strays from the central story of the conflict between man and machine.  It's nice to see Symphony X tackling a concept album of this scope, and they acquit themselves splendidly.<br /><br />But enough of all that.  Let's get to the songs already!  The standout tracks for Iconoclast are as follows; \\"Iconoclast\\", the 11 minute title track opus that starts off the album, replete with chanting, extravagant soloing and a war cry chorus; \\"The End of Innocence\\", the first single off the album, similar in tone to songs from Paradise Lost, with a blistering solo; \\"Dehumanized\\", a take-no-prisoners musical march through hostile landscapes, with brutal lyrics and guitars; \\"Heretic\\", a stomping and rousing assault on the ears, with heavy drums and thrashy guitars; \\"Children of a Faceless God\\", with an epic opening and extremely catchy rhythms; \\"Electric Messiah\\", my favorite song on the album, with a captivating chorus and simply awesome instrumental segment in the middle; and \\"Prometheus (I Am Alive)\\", a majestic scorcher of a track, with great synthesizer sequencing in the background.<br /><br />The remaining tracks are almost all amazing as well, ranging from the piano-fronted power ballad of \\"When All Is Lost\\" to \\"The Lord of Chaos\\", with its stuttering opening and slow-burn chorus.<br /><br />Needless to say, the Deluxe Edition is the only way to purchase this amazing album.  You get 2 discs, 3 extra tracks, including the aforementioned \\"The Lord of Chaos\\", and the special edition packaging.  While the extra songs aren't tied into the overall concept of the album, they are still great stand-alone tracks.  Extra Symphony X is never a bad thing IMO, so don't be afraid to fork out the extra cash.<br /><br />All in all, I would say the wait was well worth it.  Everything we've come to expect from Symphony X is present in spades on Iconoclast. Amidst the ever popular \\"some scientists believe\\", \\"Dr. So-and-so's findings indicate\\" and \\"I've observed\\", the reader might find a few interesting ideas to mull over, but you have to wade through the author's extremely wordy sentiments to find a kernel of interest.<br /><br />The truth is that in order to get published or get grants, scientists and researchers must come up with something new or they are just rewriting someone else's previous research findings.  It almost forces authors to adopt the idea that bad science is better than old science.  None of these new thoughts are necessarily true but they better fit dog owner's desire to find `bonding' experiences.<br /><br />The one item of truth readers will hopefully take from this book and other books of the same ilk is that dogs do not think like people and are not motivated by `people' emotions.  Not a single day goes by that some pet owner doesn't tell me that his dog is doing something out of spite or that his dog thinks it is the boss.  Many, many pet owners simply cannot get their minds around behavior that is so different from human.<br /><br />In spite of all the questionable research, dog's motivations are still a mystery to people.  Scientists are just now beginning to get a glimmer of one fact that they have absolutely refused to consider for decades.  Dogs are a lot smarter than the scientific community has ever given them credit for.  They do have some form of very basic reasoning.  Once researchers can consider this possibility, some progress might be made.<br /><br />One of the most thought provoking things I have read recently are \\"National Geographic's\\" features on the minds and motivations of animals.  Very brief and to the point, yet it gives readers lots of new ideas to contemplate.

B001ECQ75A|2.0|*** This review may contain spoilers ***<br /><br />When Kung Fu Panda was released in China, it was praised not only for the production values but its faithfulness to Chinese culture. Suddenly people in China were asking, how can an American production company outdo their Chinese counterparts in creating an animated film that sends such a positive message about China and gets everything right? It's akin to the Russians who launched the first man into space, only to see the American overtake them, eventually landing a man on the moon. For many Chinese critics, Kung Fu Panda is the American 'first lunar landing' of animated martial arts movies.<br /><br />But if the Chinese critics only had a bit of critical acumen, they could easily see there's nothing to be jealous about here. Sure there's a great amount of respect for Chinese culture seen throughout the film (down to the last Chinese character in the credits) as well as some sensational animation including the opening anime 'dream' sequence as well as that incredibly difficult feat of having tons of realistic looking confetti pour down at the Kung Fu tournament where the Kung Fu Panda, Po, is crowned Dragon Warrior. But how is Chinese culture honored by turning Chinese people into a bunch of goofy, self-effacing anthropomorphic animals? Surely kids under eight will dig it, but adults? I hardly think so.<br /><br />While the directors of 'Panda' are firmly ensconced in the world of martial arts and action adventure films, they are neophytes when it comes to comedy. It all comes down to one silly slapstick scene after another emphasizing Po as a good natured, bumbling fool with a decided inferiority complex. Whether he fails getting up the steps into the arena for the Kung Fu tournament, or shoots himself high into the air strapped to a bunch of fireworks and then unceremoniously falling straight down and landing inside the arena, only to be crowned Dragon Warrior, the idea that we should fall in love with his lovable loser is spelled out from the beginning and repeated ad infinitum. How many times do we have to see Po chucked around like an inflatable doll whether it's by Master Shifu, Villain Tai Lung or when he finds himself caught inside the mechanical torture chamber, a device that Shifu's Dragon Warrior candidates, 'The Furious Five', in contrast, have mastered.<br /><br />I do confess that Master Shifu's 'epiphany' in realizing that he has to train Po in a different way so that he can succeed as the dragon warrior, might have been the most clever moment in the comic arc of the film. And certainly Po's transformation from self-effacing masochist to gleeful aggressor as he embraces his 'appetite', is a welcome plot twist after being subjected to the one joke idea of Po as a bumbling clown. Yes, there's finally a method to Po's madness, as Tai Lung is unable to harness Po's new found manic energy. It's sort of like a dominant major league batter unable to master the completely unpredictable path of a knuckle ball.<br /><br />But if we view Po as representing a grownup who still acts like a kid with the particularly bad habit of overeating, then the message 'be yourself', should fall on deaf ears. In this day where smart educators are trying to control the epidemic of childhood obesity, Po's unbridled enthusiasm for that extra cupcake, would not lead to his triumph as dragon warrior. It's a wishful conceit on the part of the directors that Po would end up victorious in his confrontation with the evil Tai Lung. The filmmakers' message to kids here is be happy even if you don't treat yourself well. And things will turn out alright even if you've made no effort to change yourself.<br /><br />As for the action adventure aspect of 'Panda', I can't help it if it seems that the 'holy trinity' of Shifu, Oogway and Tai Lung, are pilfered straight out of the Star Wars play book. Shifu appears to be a Yoda stand-in with the wise, all-knowing tortoise Oogway, reprising the Obi-Wan Kenobi role (Oogway appears to employ his own version of 'The Force' as he is responsible for turning the slight Red Panda Master Shifu into a powerhouse). Finally, Tai Lung can go toe to toe with Darth Vadar any day as both have no problems mixing erudition with brute force.<br /><br />A good deal of 'Panda' is taken up by one Kung Fu sequence after another and you really have to be a martial arts fan to be enamored with all those repetitious action sequences. Less bothersome (but still silly) was the decision to make Po's father, Mr. Ping, a goose. There's also the Wuxi Finger Hold that leads to Tai Lung's destruction (at first it seems like a nuclear explosion but it really is a clearing out of 'bad energy' or 'bad karma'). Finally, The 'Furious Five' are not distinct enough as characters despite the conscription of such luminaries as Angelina Jolie and Jackie Chan to perform the voice overs.<br /><br />I think 'Kung Fu Panda' would have been a better film had the films' scenarists decided from the outset not to make Po into such a hapless buffoon. That would have also probably meant that the sentimental Mr. Ping and his love for noodles had to go too. In its present incarnation, Kung Fu Panda is certainly suitable for kids (although Tai Lung's antics might be too strong a draught for children under eight). As for adults, ignore the dopey screenplay and concentrate on the visuals and the music, which certainly deserve the accolades already garnered throughout the film community since the film's release in 2008.ion of 'The Force' as he is responsible for turning the slight Red Panda Master Shifu into a powerhouse). Finally, Tai Lung can go toe to toe with Darth Vadar any day as both have no problems mixing erudition with brute force.    A good deal of 'Panda' is taken up by one Kung Fu sequence after another and you really have to be a martial arts fan to be enamored with all those repetitious action sequences. Less bothersome (but still silly) was the decision to make Po's father, Mr. Ping, a goose. There's also the Wuxi Finger Hold that leads to Tai Lung's destruction (at first it seems like a nuclear explosion but it really is a clearing out of 'bad energy' or 'bad karma'). Finally, The 'Furious Five' are not distinct enough as characters despite the conscription of such luminaries as Angelina Jolie and Jackie Chan to perform the voice overs.     I think 'Kung Fu Panda' would have been a better film had the films' scenarists decided from the outset not to make Po into such a hapless buffoon. That would have also probably meant that the sentimental Mr. Ping and his love for noodles had to go too. In its present incarnation, Kung Fu Panda is certainly suitable for kids (although Tai Lung's antics might be too strong a draught for children under eight). As for adults, ignore the dopey screenplay and concentrate on the visuals and the music, which certainly deserve the accolades already garnered throughout the film community since the film's release in 2008.

B003XF1XOQ|4.0|Ok at first I really was a little disappointed with the way that this book was written.  While I still think it was a bit of a political propaganda in itself, I am slowly coming to understand why she wrote the way she did and I appreciate it all the more.  This book dosen't present the stoic optimism that they other ones did and you really don't get the sense leaving this book that \\"they all live happily ever after\\".  Parts of this book even border on completely depressing, but I've come to really appreciate the reality that Suzanne Collins deals in.  ****IF YOU DIDNT READ THE BOOK, STOP READING HERE***      Much like the life of a war ravaged young soldier, Katniss, Peeta, Gale, Haymitch, Annie et al. have been through war and they will never be the same for it.  This is reality.  Young men and women are going off to war every day and returning home.  We can't expect them to see the world the same way.  I was sad to see them such broken people at the end.  At first reading the epilogue gave me hope, but now after re-reading the end I think SC made a VERY large statement in not having Katniss refer to her children by their names.  She calls them the girl and \\"he\\".  It speaks volumes of how dead inside she really is if she can't even rejoice and find happiness and comfort in her family.  Its a sad reality but I felt like there was a larger message here that I give her alot of credit for.  This isn't the fairy tale that most YA books portray.  It was brave on her part because alot of people wanted the comfort of Katniss being put back together after all the tragedies.      I did think the end felt a bit rushed though and it definitely left me wanting more.  I'm hoping that when they produce the movies that they deal a bit more with the aftermath of Prim's death and the craziness that ensues in the outside world during Katniss's trial after she killed Coin.    All-in-all this was much less of a book about her BEING the Mockinjay and more about the battles itself.  Thecharacters almost seem like the subplot to the larger picture here.  Still a good read and wraps up the series fairly well.  Worth the read.

B000B8QEYC|3.0|Amarantine, unlike all of Enya's previous work, sadly underwhelmed me. It is a good listen, but, with a few exceptions, I didn't feel I was hearing anything new. Enya's albums all have a distinct `sound' to them, that which identifies them from the other albums. Not Amarantine.<br /><br />The good first. There are some great songs on Amarantine. \\"It's in the Rain\\", the haunting \\"Drifting\\" (the album's only instrumental), the furious \\"The River Sings\\", the anthemic \\"Less than a Pearl\\"... Amarantine has its winners. There's even the haiku-like \\"Sumiregusa\\", entirely in Japanese, which is a pleasant grower.<br /><br />However, there are also some tracks which sound fairly, it must be said, unspectacular. \\"A Moment Lost\\" and \\"Amid the Falling Snow\\" are both such dull and uncreative tracks that I'm surprised that Enya put them on the album at all. Even the filler songs of The Memory of Trees were wonderful. That's the other thing, what about the creativity in the instrumentation? You can really hear, in each Enya album, just judging from the sound, what album it is. Not so with Amarantine. It lacks that special sound identifiable with itself. As good as the aforementioned tracks are, Amarantine lacks that \\"wow\\" factor which her other albums sport. Normally, Enya's music transports you, tugging at your heartstrings and mesmerizing you. I missed that feeling immensely when listening to Amarantine, and it's a real shame.<br /><br />Another problem is one that lyricist Roma Ryan is responsible for. Three tracks are in the fictional language Loxian, created by Roma herself. In the booklet, each of these songs has the lyrics in Loxian as well as the English translation, which is a welcome addition. However, Loxian, while beautiful to the ears, has an alphabet completely unrelated to the Latin alphabet, making following these songs, lyrically, impossible, which is quite off-putting. There are also no songs in Enya's native Irish, which is rather sad. Irish might have made some songs sound a bit more interesting.<br /><br />For those of you wanting more elaboration, I will go deeper into each individual track.<br /><br />LESS THAN A PEARL<br />A breahttaking composition, with a moody and yet gloriously inspiring atmosphere. The chorus of vocals in the Loxian language weave a haunting layer of sound. A very strong way to open the album; definitely one of the best tracks.<br /><br />AMARANTINE<br />This is the first time the album's title track is not the opener, and perhaps it was for the best here. \\"Amarantine\\" is very plain and \\"safe\\". And the lovey-dovy lyrics just sound unimaginative on Roma Ryan's part. A sadly prophetic taste for what's to come.<br /><br />IT'S IN THE RAIN<br />This is one track I am taken with; understated and low-key but steady. The melody is strong and gripping. A moody, introspective track that has its own way of gripping the listener.<br /><br />IF I COULD BE WHERE YOU ARE<br />This song's beauty is its simplicity - it's all vocal and strings, and is a haunting thing in its own right.  It reminds me of some of Enya's b-sides like \\"I May Not Awaken\\", only more hopeful and stirring.<br /><br />THE RIVER SINGS<br />The first track I was truly taken with - an energetic, fast and feisty song with a gorgeous, thunderous sound. It's especially a pity here that you can't really follow the Loxian lyrics because it's another of the strongest tracks on the album.<br /><br />LONG LONG JOURNEY<br />Not one of the album's worst songs, but it's to thank its charm and catchiness that it gets away with its painfully unimaginative sound.<br /><br />SUMIREGUSA<br />Another winner, slow and dripping with atmosphere. The haiku-like lyrics, in Japanese, are very moving.<br /><br />SOMEONE SAID GOODBYE<br />Maybe it's just because of the pure melancholy of this song but I've never been able to really warm up to it. It divides people, but I think it's one of the best English-language tracks on the album in that it weaves a mood so far removed from what one might potentially expect from Enya's work. I see some of Enya's emotion seep through here.<br /><br />A MOMENT LOST<br />Speaking of things you can expect, this song is so plain and unimaginative that it borders on plain boring. It doesn't even sound fully composed. Waste of disc space, a dire song.<br /><br />DRIFTING<br />Now we're talking. Perhaps my biggest personal favourite; a gorgeous, pristine instrumental (which this album could definitely use more of). The cello running through helps make this melody downright goosebump-inducing. This is magic, and proves once again that Enya doesn't need lyrics if the music is strong in its own right.<br /><br />AMID THE FALLING SNOW<br />This song took me a long time to warm up to. If anything, it has a great chorus and the final thirty seconds are very appealing. But the lyrics don't tell me anything, really, and the melody is fairly dire in retrospect.<br /><br />WATER SHOWS THE HIDDEN HEART<br />For a closer, this is a fairly anticlimatic song in the sense that for such a lyric-central melody, you can't follow the lyrics in question. And the melody, while nice and soothing, isn't strong enough to be a closer. While this is a moving song, it has to be the weakest closer to any of Enya's albums.<br /><br />Amarantine is certainly Enya's poorest release, but it still has it good songs to speak of. The principle flaw as I see it is the sing-songy element in a lot of the songs; I miss the powerful melodies that were above that. As it stands we've heard it all before, and portrayed much stronger, than here. The artwork, ironically, illustrates the album perfectly; a whole lot of dull whites with some delicious reds thrown in. And Winter Came... would see Enya return to form, but Amarantine is strictly for Enya's most devoted fans.emoved from what one might potentially expect from Enya's work. B004J73WZ0|5.0|The elves in the Membran workshop have been working overtime trying to figure out how to squeeze more cash out of jaded Mahler fans like myself who thought they would never buy another \\"Resurrection\\", to say nothing of yet another complete cycle of the symphonies. When I reviewed their earlier collection of symphonies 1,2,4,5,8,9,\\"Das Lied von der Erde\\" and lieder a few months ago ([[ASIN:B000GFKUDQ Mahler: Lieder- Symphonies Nos. 1, 2, 4, 5, 8 & 9 / Das Lied von der Erde]]) , I was able to resist buying it since I had many of the recordings, but this time they got me. Even though I had a couple of these in other incarnations, the combination of low price and the mix of conductors and repertoire was too much to resist. I'm really glad I waited, since the new set contains duplicates of four performances included in the earlier set (Walter's 5th and \\"Das Lied,\\" Stokowski's 8th,Scherchen's Adagio from the 10th).     Just finding out exactly what is in this new set required a bit of research; I found out about its existence elsewhere and had to dig deep before I located Amazon's enigmatically minimalist listing. For starters, five of the performances are vintage 1950s studio recordings from Decca, RCA, and Columbia which are legendary to the longtime Mahler collector; presumably they are now in public domain. I remember Kubelik's 1st as being one of the relatively few available Mahler recordings on LP back in the early 1960s before  the Mahler recording juggernaut picked up speed. Reiner's 4th, which I believe to be the only stereo recording in the present set, has always been among the top recommendations. Walter's 5th has been reissued numerous times on CD by Sony since I first encountered it on an old Odyssey LP. The Vienna \\"Das Lied von der Erde,\\" the second of Walter's three studio recordings, has long been famed for contralto Kathleen Ferrier's participation (Ferrier is  also heard here in the live recordings of the 2nd and 3rd). The Scherchen 10th has been available on DGG and Westminster. There are very enthusiastic Amazon reviews for almost everything here by knowledgeable Mahlerites; the Klemperer 2nd and the Mitropoulos 6th in particular have been highly praised. The Boult 3rd appears to be the same performance issued by Testament; the Stokowski 8th has been issued by Music and Arts and others. I wondered whether or not the Rosbaud 7th was the famous performance on the old Vox LP set which an old \\"High Fidelity\\" magazine reviewer trashed as a disservice to the conductor's memory, but that was with the Berlin Radio orchestra;this Sudwestfunk/Baden-Baden reading appears to have been issued with the 9th on a now out-of-print Andromeda 3-disc set.    Here are the nuts and bolts:     Disc 1 (78 minutes) Symphony no.1  Kubelik, Vienna Philharmonic (1953), Symphony no.10, 1st movement Scherchen, Vienna State Opera Orch (1952).  Disc 2 (72 minutes) Symphony no.2 Klemperer; Jo Vincent, Kathleen Ferrier, Concertgebouw (1951, live)  Disc 3 (72 minutes) Symphony no.3 Mvts. 1-5  Boult; Kathleen Ferrier, BBC Symphony (1947,live)  Disc 4 (79 Minutes) Symphony no.3 cont'd, Mvt. 6 ; Symphony No.4  Reiner, Lisa Della Casa, Chicago Symphony Orch. (1958)  Disc 5 (61 minutes) Symphony no.5  Walter, New York Philharmonic (1947)  Disc 6 (73 minutes) Symphony no.6  Mitropoulos, New York Philharmonic (1955, live)  Disc 7 (77 minutes) Symphony no.7  Rosbaud, Symphony Orch. Sudwestfunk Baden-Baden (1957,live)  Disc 8 (78 minutes) Symphony no.8  Stokowski; Frances Yeend,Uta Graf, Camilla Williams, Martha Lipton,Louise Bernhardt, Eugene Conley, Carlos Alexander, George London  New York Philharmonic(1950,live)  Disc 9  (75 minutes) Rosbaud, Symphony Orch. Sudwestfunk Baden-Baden (1954,live)  Disc 10 (60 minutes) Das Lied von der Erde Walter, Julius Patzak, Kathleen Ferrier, Vienna Philharmonic 1952    Hardcore Mahler fans will recognize that most of these are mono recordings and will not be the last word in sound quality, but none of them are unlistenable and most of the live recordings have no appreciable audience noise. The ten discs are boxed in individual paper sleeves in a handy shelf-space saving box. No notes, just timings and track listings. If you're listening on the computer, many of the track listings are mislabeled; fortunately the printed listings on the sleeves are correct.    Bottom line:   If you're not very familiar with Mahler your first recordings should be in modern sound; DGG and EMI have very tempting 'complete edition' sets, Solti and Bernstein's cycles can be had at very reasonable prices. If you want a second set of the symphonies with some of the most justly famed interpreters of the older generation, this one is a no-brainer even at full price and an outright steal at marketplace rates; I got my copy for around 15 bucks including postage.

Glimpses is also a good collection for new fans, because it contains stories, which reveal several interesting things about the characters and their lives.<br /><br />If somebody hasn't read any Nightrunner books yet, here's some information about them:<br /><br />The Nightrunner series currently consists of five books:<br />- Luck in the Shadows (1996)<br />- Stalking Darkness (1997)<br />- Traitor's Moon (1999)<br />- Shadows Return (2008)<br />- The White Road (2010)<br /><br />There will be at last two more books. The forthcoming sixth book is currently called The Summer Players (2011), but it's a working title, so the title may change before publication.<br /><br />The main characters are Seregil and Alec. Seregil is a master thief and spy, who's known as the \\"Rh&iacute;minee Cat\\". Alec is a young man, who becomes friends with Seregil, when Seregil rescues him. They work secretly for Skala and do nightrunning (they steal certain items etc). Their glorious adventures take them to different places and difficult situations.<br /><br />Nightrunner books are entertaining, macabre and beautifully written fantasy books for adult readers. These books have become famous for the gradually developing homosexual relationship between Seregil and Alec, but there's much more to these books than that.<br /><br />There are several different kind of elements in these books: political intrigue, mysteries, war, magic, friendship and love. Although Lynn Flewelling's books contain dark happenings, there's also plenty of good and clever humour. These books are the best kind of fantasy entertainment for adult readers who appreciate fine prose, fantastic adventures, mysteries and macabre happenings - these books are fun to read.<br /><br />Now I'll write more about Glimpses:<br /><br />Glimpses contains the following stories (and an excerpt from the forthcoming sixth Nightrunner book):<br />- Misfit<br />- The Wild<br />- By the River<br />- The Bond<br /><br />Misfit: Misfit is a story about how Seregil becomes Nysander's apprentice in Rh&iacute;minee. It also tells about Seregil's first Skalan sexual liaison (everybody who's read Shadows Return will be thrilled to read this story, because it tells more about Seregil's affair with a certain person). It was interesting to read about Seregil, Korathan and Phoria, when they were young.<br /><br />The Wild: The Wild is a story about Alec's childhood (Alec is six years old), but it's also a story about how his parents met each other and what happened to their relationship. In this story Alec's father, Amasa, meets Alec's mother, Ireya, who's a HÃ¢zadriÃ«lfaie. The Wild is a beautiful, moving and brutal story.<br /><br />By the River: By the River tells how Seregil and Micum Cavish meet. Their meeting is interesting, because Micum first thinks that Seregil is a girl, but soon finds out that he's an AurÃ«nfaie. In this story Micum is younger than in the books.<br /><br />The Bond: The Bond is a story about Seregil and Alec's first night as lovers. This short story takes place at the end of Stalking Darkness, when Seregil is an emotional wreck after the tragic death of the wizard Nysander. The Bond is an interesting and beautiful story about Seregil and Alec's bonding (tal&iacute;menios bond) and their deepening relationship. It's an emotionally satisfying story.<br /><br />It may be a surprise to readers that this collection contains erotic scenes. I knew that there would be erotic scenes in this collection, because Lynn Flewelling mentioned about it in her blog,but I never expected that she'd be this bold. The erotic scenes (there are homoerotic scenes in The Bond and Misfit, and there's one erotic scene in The Wild) are quite graphic, but in my opinion they aren't exactly hardcore erotica, because Lynn Flewelling writes about real sex. She uses nice graphic details, but the scenes are erotic, sensual and beautiful (and feelings are involved). She writes fluently and lovingly about sexual situations without preaching.<br /><br />All these stories are fascinating and well written. It's a pleasure to read this kind of fantasy prose, because Lynn Flewelling never lets her fans down. She writes exquisitely about different and difficult things (I'm sure that all readers will agree with me on this when I say that she's one of the best fantasy writers ever). I'm sure that her addictive writing style and storytelling talents will charm all the readers.<br /><br />I have to say that I like Lynn Flewelling's characters very much, because they're realistic, three-dimensional and memorable characters with their own feelings and traits. They're almost like real living and breathing people - that's why it's so easy to like them.<br /><br />Foreword by Laura Anne Gilman was an interesting essay. I also enjoyed reading \\"A Few Words\\" by Lynn Flewelling, because she wrote about the stories and illustrations. The excerpt from the forthcoming book was also interesting and it made me want to read the book as soon as it's published.<br /><br />The cover art by Anne Cain looks gorgeous and the black and white illustrations by professional and amateur artists are beautiful. I like the illustrations very much and I think that other Nightrunner fans will also like them.<br /><br />Three Crow Press has done a good job with this short story collection. It's great that these excellent short stories have been gathered into one book, because it's nice to read about the moments, which have so far been only hinted at in the books.<br /><br />If you're a fan of Lynn Flewelling's Nightrunner series, this collection belongs to your bookshelf. You won't regret buying it, because the stories are charming and fascinating. This collection can also be recommended to new Flewelling fans, who haven't read all the Nightrunner books yet. Highly recommended!<br /><br />(Hint: If you haven't discovered the magic of Lynn Flewelling yet, now would be a good time to start reading her books.)te graphic, but in my opinion they aren't exactly hardcore erotica, because Lynn Flewelling writes about real sex. B002ZG994U|5.0|The A-Team was one of my TV serials from the 1980s, along with Airwolf, Quantum Leap, Tales of the Gold Monkey, etc... that mixed adventure, action, drama and romance with a large, mostly male cast full of bad guys in high places and good guys in secret hideouts.  Few made it to the big screen, and many thought the A-team would never get a full Hollywood production.  But it did, and it came out very well.  Featuring the same characters as the original show, though updated to take place in the decade of Operation Iraqi Freedom, this movie is full of plot twists, character twists, gunfights, fistfights, ludicrous dialog, ridiculous action scenes, and all of the familiar hallmarks of the TV series, except overblown in every way possible.  The casting was spot on for all the members of the team, along with all the villains of the movie.  Clocking in at just around two hours in length, the movie has several climatic action scenes, hence moving the story along at a brisk pace.  Who are the villains in the movie?  Well, they are a combination of all the villains of the TV serial; drug lords, rogue military brass, the CIA, private military contractors, etc...  Overall, a great movie worth multiple viewings.  Probably the best part of this film is how it shows the origins of B.A. Baracus' fear of flying.

0811870413|5.0|Stumbling upon Tartine Bread at my local library, I borrowed and read it, more quickly and thoroughly than I usually do with something picked up on impulse, and was inspired.    As someone who never made a dough of any kind before I assure you that even a determined newbie can turn out very acceptable loaves of Peterson's master recipe - the \\"basic country loaf\\" - in a matter of days; yet spend weeks on this one introductory lesson getting properly acquainted with capably producing a naturally leavened loaf.  I progressed quickly to the rye variation and a very good pizza crust.  I'll continue to work with this book (which I've now purchased), even as I move on to others.    This is not the compendium on bread that Hamelman's book (which I am now tackling as well) is.  I spoke with a very good professional baker (you'd probably know the name) about Peterson's method, and he mentioned that Jim Lahey from Sullivan Street Bakery is the guy who came up with the dutch oven approach employed for the country loaf.  Read Lahey's \\"My Bread\\" and you'll see a very similar product (editorially) and approach (tutorially).    Having purchased Tartine Bread, I'll surely own Hamelman & Lahey's books before long, and I'll mention Peter Reinhart's books along the way.  Perhaps I'll graduate to Calvel someday.  In the meantime, my foodie friends are quite complimentary of both my basic country loaf and the rye version, and I'm looking forward to producing Peterson's baguette soon.
